1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)

# Task: Kubernetes client-go Systems Guide

**Repository:** github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0 (mirror of github.com/sg-evals)/kubernetes--8c9c67c0 (mirror of kubernetes/kubernetes) (stripped snapshot)
**Output:** Write your document to `/workspace/documentation.md`

## Objective

Produce an advanced systems guide for client-go that explains API access, controller loops, and extension paths.

## Scope

Focus on the client-go subsystem.
Your document must explain component responsibilities, end-to-end flow, and extension or operational tradeoffs.

## Content Expectations

Address all of the following in your own structure:
- subsystem purpose, boundaries, and upstream/downstream dependencies
- key components and how responsibilities are split
- end-to-end control/request flow with concrete repository evidence
- failure modes, limits, and design tradeoffs
- extension points, customization hooks, and associated risks
- a concise map of the most important files/directories used in your analysis

## Quality Bar

- Cite concrete file paths from the repository.
- Explain behavior and interactions, not just API signatures.
- Include at least one section on operational implications (performance, reliability, or maintainability).
- Do not fabricate classes, functions, or files.

## Anti-Requirements

- Do not output `doc.go`.
- Do not provide shallow bullet dumps without system flow explanation.
- Do not rely on external docs not present in the workspace.

It describes the\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n5: \n6: ## Client Configuration\n7: \n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n13: \n14: ## REST Client\n15: \n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\n19: \n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\n23: is injected into requests.\n24: \n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\n28: \n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\n30: \n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the abili

     1→# Client-Go Systems Guide: Advanced Architecture and Operations
     2→
     3→## Executive Summary
     4→
     5→`client-go` is the official Go client library for Kubernetes, providing both typed and dynamic APIs for accessing and manipulating cluster state. The system is architected around two critical design principles: (1) **separation of concerns** between configuration, transport, and API access layers, and (2) **event-driven caching** for efficient controller implementations. This guide explains how these layers interact, what tradeoffs shape their design, and where you can extend or customize behavior.
     6→
     7→---
     8→
     9→## 1. Subsystem Purpose, Boundaries, and Dependencies
    10→
    11→### 1.1 Purpose
    12→
    13→`client-go` is a thin, opinionated wrapper over HTTP that provides:
    14→
    15→- **Configuration Management**: Loading and merging kubeconfig files with support for multiple authentication backends
    16→- **REST-Friendly API Access**: HTTP client abstractions over Kubernetes' RESTful API
    17→- **Strongly-Typed Client Interfaces**: Code-generated clientsets for type safety at compile-time
    18→- **Dynamic Client Support**: Generic interfaces for ad-hoc, unstructured object manipulation
    19→- **Controller Machinery**: Event-driven caching and work queue patterns for building reconciliation loops
    20→- **Discovery**: Introspection of available API groups and resources on a cluster
    21→- **Leader Election**: Distributed locking for single-writer semantics in high-availability scenarios
    22→
    23→### 1.2 Boundaries and Scope
    24→
    25→Client-go **does not**:
    26→- Manage the cluster itself (that is apiserver's job)
    27→- Guarantee strong consistency (it is eventually consistent)
    28→- Provid

# Client-Go Systems Guide: Advanced Architecture and Operations

## Executive Summary

`client-go` is the official Go client library for Kubernetes, providing both typed and dynamic APIs for accessing and manipulating cluster state. The system is architected around two critical design principles: (1) **separation of concerns** between configuration, transport, and API access layers, and (2) **event-driven caching** for efficient controller implementations. This guide explains how these layers interact, what tradeoffs shape their design, and where you can extend or customize behavior.

---

## 1. Subsystem Purpose, Boundaries, and Dependencies

### 1.1 Purpose

`client-go` is a thin, opinionated wrapper over HTTP that provides:

- **Configuration Management**: Loading and merging kubeconfig files with support for multiple authentication backends
- **REST-Friendly API Access**: HTTP client abstractions over Kubernetes' RESTful API
- **Strongly-Typed Client Interfaces**: Code-generated clientsets for type safety at compile-time
- **Dynamic Client Support**: Generic interfaces for ad-hoc, unstructured object manipulation
- **Controller Machinery**: Event-driven caching and work queue patterns for building reconciliation loops
- **Discovery**: Introspection of available API groups and resources on a cluster
95-assistanttext-
Excellent! I've successfully created a comprehensive **Client-Go Systems Guide** that addresses all requirements. Here's a summary of what the document covers:

## Document Coverage

### ✅ Subsystem Purpose, Boundaries, and Dependencies (Section 1)
- Clear purpose definition with 7 key responsibilities
- Explicit boundaries (what client-go does and doesn't do)
- ASCII diagram showing upstream/downstream relationships
- Dependencies on apimachinery, API types, auth plugins

### ✅ Key Components & Responsibility Distribution (Section 2)
All major components with concrete file references:
- **Configuration layer**: `tools/clientcmd/loader.go`, `rest/config.go`
- **REST client**: `rest/client.go`, `transport/round_trippers.go`
- **Typed APIs**: `kubernetes/clientset.go`, generated typed clients
- **Dynamic API**: `dynamic/simple.go`
- **Discovery**: `discovery/discovery_client.go`
- **Controller machinery**: Reflector, DeltaFIFO, Indexer, SharedInformer
- **Work Queue**: `util/workqueue/queue.go`
- **Leader Election**: `tools/leaderelection/leaderelection.go`

### ✅ End-to-End Control Flows (Section 3)
Three detailed flows with concrete file references and execution steps:
- **Typed API + Informer flow**: From kubeconfig → REST client → Reflector → DeltaFIFO → EventHandlers → WorkQueue → Reconciliation
- **Dynamic client flow**: For CRDs and generic operations
- **Leader election flow**: HA setup with three replicas competing for Lease

### ✅ Failure Modes, Limits, and Tradeoffs (Section 4)
- Resource version expiration and recovery (bookmarks, re-LIST)
- Watch timeout and jitter to prevent thundering herd
- Client-side rate limiting and backoff
- DeltaFIFO event loss prevention
- Mutation detection for debugging
- Resync period for eventual consistency

  "content": "{\"content\":\"1: # `client-go` Architecture\\n2: \\n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\\n5: \\n6: ## Client Configuration\\n7: \\n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\\n13: \\n14: ## REST Client\\n15: \\n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\\n19: \\n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\\n23: is injected into requests.\\n24: \\n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\\n28: \\n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\\n30: \\n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \\n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\\n46: \\n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\\n48: \\n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\\n57: \\n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\\n59: \\n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\\n61: models.\\n62: \\n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\\n64: \\n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\\n71: \\n72: ## Code Generation\\n73: \\n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\\n77: several key components:\\n78: \\n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\\n83: \\n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\\n86: \\n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\\n88: \\n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\\n90: \\n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\\n93: \\n94: The data flow is as follows:\\n95: \\n96: ```mermaid\\n97: graph TD\\n98:     subgraph \\\"Kubernetes API\\\"\\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\\n100:     end\\n101: \\n102:     subgraph \\\"client-go: Informer Mechanism\\\"\\n103:         Reflector(\\\"1. Reflector\\\")\\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\\\"2. DeltaFIFO\\\")\\n105:         Indexer[\\\"3. Indexer (Cache)\\\"]\\n106:         EventHandlers(\\\"4. Event Handlers\\\")\\n107:     end\\n108: \\n109:     subgraph \\\"User Code\\\"\\n110:         WorkQueue[\\\"5. Work Queue\\\"]\\n111:         Controller(\\\"6. Controller\\\")\\n112:     end\\n113: \\n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\\n121: ```\\n122: \\n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\\n126: **\\\"too old\\\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\\n129: \\n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\\n132: \\n133: ## Controller Patterns\\n134: \\n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\\n136: ensure resiliency.\\n137: \\n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\\n142: \\n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\\n145: shared `Lease` object.\\n146: \\n147: ## Server-Side Apply\\n148: \\n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\\n153: construct the declarative patch.\\n154: \\n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\\n156: \\n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\\n159: \\n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\\n163: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md\"}",

    "content": "1: # `client-go` Architecture\n2: \n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n5: \n6: ## Client Configuration\n7: \n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n13: \n14: ## REST Client\n15: \n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\n19: \n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\n23: is injected into requests.\n24: \n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\n28: \n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\n30: \n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\n46: \n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\n48: \n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\n57: \n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\n59: \n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\n61: models.\n62: \n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\n64: \n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\n71: \n72: ## Code Generation\n73: \n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\n77: several key components:\n78: \n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\n83: \n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\n86: \n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\n88: \n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\n90: \n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\n93: \n94: The data flow is as follows:\n95: \n96: ```mermaid\n97: graph TD\n98:     subgraph \"Kubernetes API\"\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\n100:     end\n101: \n102:     subgraph \"client-go: Informer Mechanism\"\n103:         Reflector(\"1. Reflector\")\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\"2. DeltaFIFO\")\n105:         Indexer[\"3. Indexer (Cache)\"]\n106:         EventHandlers(\"4. Event Handlers\")\n107:     end\n108: \n109:     subgraph \"User Code\"\n110:         WorkQueue[\"5. Work Queue\"]\n111:         Controller(\"6. Controller\")\n112:     end\n113: \n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\n121: ```\n122: \n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\n126: **\"too old\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\n129: \n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\n132: \n133: ## Controller Patterns\n134: \n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\n136: ensure resiliency.\n137: \n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\n142: \n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\n145: shared `Lease` object.\n146: \n147: ## Server-Side Apply\n148: \n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\n153: construct the declarative patch.\n154: \n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\n156: \n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\n159: \n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\n163: ",

          | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | \u2713               |\\n91: | `HEAD`                        | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              |\\n92: \\n93: Key:\\n94: \\n95: * `\u2713` Exactly the same features / API objects in both client-go and the Kubernetes\\n96:   version.\\n97: * PRs that are\n50: cherry-picked into an older Kubernetes release branch will result in an update\n51: to the corresponding branch in `client-go`, with a corresponding new tag\n52: changing the patch version.\n53: \n54: #### Branches and tags.\n55: \n56: We will create a new branch and tag for each increment in the minor version\n57: number. We will create only a new tag for each increment in the patch\n58: version number. See [semver](http://semver.org/) for definitions of major,\n59: minor, and patch.\n60: \n61: The HEAD of the master branch in client-go will track the HEAD of the master\n62: branch in the main Kubernetes repo.\n63: \n64: #### Compatibility: your code <-> client-go\n65: \n66: The `v0.x.y` tags indicate that go APIs may change in incompatible ways in\n67: different versions.\n68: \n69: See [INSTALL.md](INSTALL.md) for guidelines on requiring a specific\n70: version of client-go.\n71: \n72: #### Compatibility: client-go <-> Kubernetes clusters\n73: \n74: Since Kubernetes is backwards compatible with clients, older `client-go`\n75: versions will work with many different Kubernetes cluster versions.\n76: \n77: We will backport bugfixes--but not new features--into older versions   "content": "{\"content\":\"1: # client-go under the hood\\n2: \\n3: The [client-go](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/) library contains various mechanisms that you can use when\\n4: developing your custom controllers. These mechanisms are defined in the\\n5: [tools/cache folder](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/tree/master/tools/cache) of the library.\\n6: \\n7: Here is a pictorial representation showing how the various components in\\n8: the client-go library work and their interaction points with the custom\\n9: controller code that you will write.\\n10: \\n11: <p align=\\\"center\\\">\\n12:   <img src=\\\"images/client-go-controller-interaction.jpeg\\\" height=\\\"600\\\" width=\\\"700\\\"/>\\n13: </p>\\n14: \\n15: ## client-go components\\n16: \\n17: * Reflector: A reflector, which is defined in [type *Reflector* inside package *cache*](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/tools/cache/reflector.go),\\n18: watches the Kubernetes API for the specified resource type (kind).\\n19: The function in which this is done is *ListAndWatch*.\\n20: The watch could be for an in-built resource or it could be for a custom resource.\\n21: When the reflector receives notification about existence of new\\n22: resource instance through the watch API, it gets the newly created object\\n23: using the corresponding listing API and puts it in the Delta Fifo queue\\n24: inside the *watchHandler* function.\\n25: \\n26: \\n27: * Informer: An informer defined in the [base controller inside package *cache*](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/tools/cache/controller.go) pops objects from the Delta Fifo queue.\\n28: The function in which this is done is *processLoop*. The job of this base controller\\n29: is to save the object for later retrieval, and to invoke our controller passing it the object.\\n30: \\n31: * Indexer: An indexer provides indexing functionality over objects.\\n32: It is defined in [type *Indexer* inside package *cache*](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/tools/cache/index.go). A typical indexing use-case is to create an index based on object labels. Indexer can\\n33: maintain indexes based on several indexing functions.\\n34: Indexer uses a thread-safe data store to store objects and their keys.\\n35: There is a default function named *MetaNamespaceKeyFunc* defined in [type *Store* inside package *cache*](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/tools/cache/store.go)\\n36: that generates an object\u2019s key as `<namespace>/<name>` combination for that object.\\n37: \\n38: \\n39: ## Custom Controller components\\n40: \\n41: * Informer reference: This is the reference to the Informer instance that knows\\n42: how to work with your custom resource objects. Your custom controller code needs\\n43: to create the appropriate Informer.\\n44: \\n45: * Indexer reference: This is the reference to the Indexer instance that knows\\n46: how to work with your custom resource objects. Your custom controller code needs\\n47: to create this. You will be using this reference for retrieving objects for\\n48: later processing.\\n49: \\n50: The base controller in client-go provides the *NewIndexerInformer* function to create Informer and Indexer.\\n51: In your code you can either [directly invoke this function](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/examples/workqueue/main.go#L174) or [use factory methods for creating an informer.](https://github.com/kubernetes/sample-controller/blob/master/main.go#L61)\\n52: \\n53: * Resource Event Handlers: These are the callback functions which will be called by\\n54: the Informer when it wants to deliver an object to your controller. The typical\\n55: pattern to write these functions is to obtain the dispatched object\u2019s key\\n56: and enqueue that key in a work queue for further processing.\\n57: \\n58: * Work queue: This is the queue that you create in your controller code to decouple\\n59: delivery of an object from its processing. Resource event handler functions are written\\n60: to extract the delivered object\u2019s key and add that to the work queue.\\n61: \\n62: * Process Item: This is the function that you create in your code which processes items\\n63: from the work queue. There can be one or more other functions that do the actual processing.\\n64: These functions will typically use the [Indexer reference](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/examples/workqueue/main.go#L73), or a Listing wrapper to retrieve the object corresponding to the key.\",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/sample-controller/docs/controller-client-go.md\"}",
  "structuredContent": {
    "content": "1: # client-go under the hood\n2: \n3: The [client-go](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/) library contains various mechanisms that you can use when\n4: developing your custom controllers. These mechanisms are defined in the\n5: [tools/cache folder](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/tree/master/tools/cache) of the library.\n6: \n7: Here is a pictorial representation showing how the various components in\n8: the client-go library work and their interaction points with the custom\n9: controller code that you will write.\n10: \n11: <p align=\"center\">\n12:   <img src=\"images/client-go-controller-interaction.jpeg\" height=\"600\" width=\"700\"/>\n13: </p>\n14: \n15: ## client-go components\n16: \n17: * Reflector: A reflector, which is defined in [type *Reflector* inside package *cache*](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/tools/cache/reflector.go),\n18: watches the Kubernetes API for the specified resource type (kind).\n19: The function in which this is done is *ListAndWatch*.\n20: The watch could be for an in-built resource or it could be for a custom resource.\n21: When the reflector receives notification about existence of new\n22: resource instance through the watch API, it gets the newly created object\n23: using the corresponding listing API and puts it in the Delta Fifo queue\n24: inside the *watchHandler* function.\n25: \n26: \n27: * Informer: An informer defined in the [base controller inside package *cache*](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/tools/cache/controller.go) pops objects from the Delta Fifo queue.\n28: The function in which this is done is *processLoop*. The job of this base controller\n29: is to save the object for later retrieval, and to invoke our controller passing it the object.\n30: \n31: * Indexer: An indexer provides indexing functionality over objects.\n32: It is defined in [type *Indexer* inside package *cache*](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/tools/cache/index.go). A typical indexing use-case is to create an index based on object labels. Indexer can\n33: maintain indexes based on several indexing functions.\n34: Indexer uses a thread-safe data store to store objects and their keys.\n35: There is a default function named *MetaNamespaceKeyFunc* defined in [type *Store* inside package *cache*](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/tools/cache/store.go)\n36: that generates an object\u2019s key as `<namespace>/<name>` combination for that object.\n37: \n38: \n39: ## Custom Controller components\n40: \n41: * Informer reference: This is the reference to the Informer instance that knows\n42: how to work with your custom resource objects. Your custom controller code needs\n43: to create the appropriate Informer.\n44: \n45: * Indexer reference: This is the reference to the Indexer instance that knows\n46: how to work with your custom resource objects. Your custom controller code needs\n47: to create this. You will be using this reference for retrieving objects for\n48: later processing.\n49: \n50: The base controller in client-go provides the *NewIndexerInformer* function to create Informer and Indexer.\n51: In your code you can either [directly invoke this function](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/examples/workqueue/main.go#L174) or [use factory methods for creating an informer.](https://github.com/kubernetes/sample-controller/blob/master/main.go#L61)\n52: \n53: * Resource Event Handlers: These are the callback functions which will be called by\n54: the Informer when it wants to deliver an object to your controller. The typical\n55: pattern to write these functions is to obtain the dispatched object\u2019s key\n56: and enqueue that key in a work queue for further processing.\n57: \n58: * Work queue: This is the queue that you create in your controller code to decouple\n59: delivery of an object from its processing. Resource event handler functions are written\n60: to extract the delivered object\u2019s key and add that to the work queue.\n61: \n62: * Process Item: This is the function that you create in your code which processes items\n63: from the work queue. There can be one or more other functions that do the actual processing.\n64: These functions will typically use the [Indexer reference](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/examples/workqueue/main.go#L73), or a Listing wrapper to retrieve the object corresponding to the key.",
